Perception of States: Why They Cannot Be Compared in Isolation
Razi explains that states are not perceived alone, so comparisons are invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that states (ahwal) cannot be compared in terms of similarity or difference because they are not perceived alone.

Core Idea
States are never perceived alone; thus, comparisons of similarity are logically impossible.

Razi begins by clarifying a fundamental point: neither the essence nor the state can be perceived in isolation. Since states are always perceived together with something else, we cannot isolate a shared aspect to judge two states as similar or different. This passage lays the logical groundwork for refuting those who treat states as independent entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Razi's argument once without analysis. Notice his rhetorical question.
2
Identify the core claim
Razi claims that both the essence and the state are not perceived alone. This is the foundation.
3
Understand why comparison fails
Because states are perceived with something else, the shared aspect is not isolated, so we cannot judge similarity/difference.
4
Connect to opponents' methodology
Razi mentions that opponents say the intellect points to a shared aspect or not, but states are not pointed to alone.
This shows the opponents' own rule does not apply to states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانه : أن الذات هي لا تدرك بالانفراد والحال لا تدرك بالانفراد ، فكيف يكون المدرك من كل حاله و المدرك من حال آخر إذا كان كل حال يدرك مع شيء آخر والمشترك ليس بمدرك بالانفراد حتى يحكم بأن المدرك من أحدهما هو المدرك من الآخر أو ليس وأنتم قاتم كل أمرين يشير العقل إليهما فإما أن يكون المنصور منهما واحداً أولا يكون والحال ليس بأمر يشير العقل إليه إشارة لا يكون إلى غيره معه . وأما دفع صاحب الـ كتاب الإلزام غير مثبتي الأحوال بأن الحال صفة سلبية لا يقتضى اشتراكا في أمر ثبوتى بين الأحوال ، فليس بدافع عنهم ، لأنهم يقولون بأن الحال سلب محض بل يقولون إنها وصف ليس ؟ وجود ايسر بحال ، فإذا الحال يشتمل عندهم على معنى غير سلب الوجود والعدم يختص ذلك ولا معدوم ، مع الأمور التى يسمونها حالا وتشترك الأحوال فيه ولكونها غير مدركة بانفرادها لا يحكمون عليها بالتماثل والاختلاف ، وقد ظهر أن ذلك الدفع منهم لم يكن بمقتض لذلك البناء على نفسه
Its explanation: that the essence (ad-dhat) is not perceived alone, and the state (al-hal) is not perceived alone. So how can what is perceived from each state and what is perceived from another state [be compared] when each state is perceived with something else, and the shared [aspect] is not perceived alone so that it can be judged that what is perceived from one is what is perceived from the other or not? And you [opponents] say of every two things that the intellect points to, either that intended [aspect] from them is one or not, but the state is not a thing that the intellect points to alone without something else with it. As for the refutation by the author of the book of the objection against the non-proponents of states—that the state is a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya) that does not require a sharing in something positive among states—it is not a defense for them, because they say that the state is pure negation (salb mahd). Rather they say it is a description that is neither existence nor non-existence, and so the state includes, according to them, a meaning other than the negation of existence and non-existence that is specific to that [state] and is not non-existent, along with the things they call states, and the states share in it. And because they are not perceived alone, they do not judge them by similarity and difference. And it has become clear that that defense on their part was not consistent with that foundation.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an explanation (bayan) and a refutation of the opponents' defense. Razi first explains that neither the essence (dhat) nor the state (hal) is perceived in isolation. Therefore, it is impossible to compare what is perceived from one state with another because each state is always perceived with something else. The shared aspect between states is not perceived alone, so one cannot judge whether the perceived aspect from two states is the same or different. The intellect cannot point to a state alone without reference to something else. Then Razi addresses the 'author of the book's' refutation (perhaps the Muhaqqiq or the commentator) who argued that the state is a negative attribute and thus does not require a positive shared aspect. Razi says this defense fails because the proponents of states do not consider the state as pure negation; rather, they consider it as something that is neither existent nor non-existent, i.e., it has a positive connotation distinct from mere negation. The states share in that connotation, but because they are not perceived alone, they are not judged as similar or different. Thus the opponents' attempt to refute the objection by reducing states to pure negation contradicts their own doctrine.

Key Terms
إدراك
انفراد
ذات
حال
تماثل
اختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That states can be compared in isolation to determine similarity or difference.
Correction: Razi shows that states are never perceived alone, so the shared aspect is not isolated; thus, similarity/difference judgments are impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why can't we compare two states in terms of similarity?
A) Because states do not exist
B) Because states are not perceived alone
C) Because the intellect cannot grasp states
D) Because all states are identical

Key Takeaway
States cannot be compared in isolation because they are never perceived alone; thus, any judgment of similarity or difference between states is unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily judgments of people or situations, do you isolate a single 'state' (e.g., a flaw or a virtue) and ignore the whole? How does Razi’s argument expose the danger of such isolation? What will you change today in how you perceive others?
?
When you compare yourself to someone else, which 'state' do you fixate on? Can you truly separate that state from the rest of their being? How does this lesson call you to abandon superficial comparisons and see yourself and others as integrated wholes?
?
Reflect on your understanding of Allah’s attributes: have you ever thought of His mercy independently of His justice? Razi’s argument warns against isolating attributes. How does this compel you to rethink your worship and trust in Allah’s complete and inseparable nature?

Refuting the Defense: States Are Not Pure Negation
Razi refutes the opponent's defense that states are purely negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi refutes the opponent's claim that states are purely negative attributes, and how this defense contradicts their own doctrine.

Core Idea
The opponent's defense fails because they actually consider states as more than pure negation.

After establishing that states are not perceived alone, Razi turns to a defense raised by the 'author of the book' on behalf of those who do not affirm states. The defense claims that states are negative attributes and therefore do not require a shared positive aspect. Razi shows this defense is inconsistent with what the proponents actually say about states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Razi's refutation once. Note the structure: statement of defense, then critique.
2
Identify the opponent's defense
The defense says states are negative attributes (sifa salbiyya) so no positive sharing is required.
3
Understand Razi's counter
Razi points out that proponents do not say states are pure negation; they say states are neither existent nor non-existent.
This implies a positive meaning beyond negation.
4
Grasp the implication
Since states share that meaning, and because they are not perceived alone, similarity/difference judgments are not made.
5
Conclusion: defense fails
The defense contradicts the proponents' own foundation, so it is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانه : أن الذات هي لا تدرك بالانفراد والحال لا تدرك بالانفراد ، فكيف يكون المدرك من كل حاله و المدرك من حال آخر إذا كان كل حال يدرك مع شيء آخر والمشترك ليس بمدرك بالانفراد حتى يحكم بأن المدرك من أحدهما هو المدرك من الآخر أو ليس وأنتم قاتم كل أمرين يشير العقل إليهما فإما أن يكون المنصور منهما واحداً أولا يكون والحال ليس بأمر يشير العقل إليه إشارة لا يكون إلى غيره معه . وأما دفع صاحب الـ كتاب الإلزام غير مثبتي الأحوال بأن الحال صفة سلبية لا يقتضى اشتراكا في أمر ثبوتى بين الأحوال ، فليس بدافع عنهم ، لأنهم يقولون بأن الحال سلب محض بل يقولون إنها وصف ليس ؟ وجود ايسر بحال ، فإذا الحال يشتمل عندهم على معنى غير سلب الوجود والعدم يختص ذلك ولا معدوم ، مع الأمور التى يسمونها حالا وتشترك الأحوال فيه ولكونها غير مدركة بانفرادها لا يحكمون عليها بالتماثل والاختلاف ، وقد ظهر أن ذلك الدفع منهم لم يكن بمقتض لذلك البناء على نفسه

The Doctrine of al-Hal (State) Explained
Imam Razi defines two types of states and states two positions on existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of states (al-hal) and the positions of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila on whether existence is essence or attribute.

Core Idea
States are either caused or uncaused, and this debate affects whether non-existents are things.

Imam Razi begins by presenting the doctrine of al-hal as held by some theologians. He distinguishes between two types of states and clarifies the disagreement over the nature of existence. This background is essential before we can appreciate his refutation in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two categories of states introduced.
Focus on the terms 'caused state' and 'uncaused state'.
2
Identify the two types of states
Find and understand the difference between a state caused by an existing attribute (like knowledge causing being knowledgeable) and an uncaused state (like the blackness of black itself).
The uncaused state is inherent to the essence.
3
Understand the disagreement on existence
Note that Ash'aris say existence is the essence itself, while Mu'tazila say it is an attribute. This leads to the claim that non-existents are 'things' (exist in some way).
This sets up the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفريع على القول بالحال
قالوا ثبوت الحالية للشيء إما أن يكون معللا بموجود قائم بذلك الشيء كالعالمية المعللة بالعلم أو لا يكون كذلك كسوادية السواد، والأول هو الحال المعلل والثاني الحال الغير المعلل، واتفقوا على أن الموجودات متساوية في الذوات ومختلفة في هذه الأحوال. وأما الوجود فزعم مثبتو الحال منا أنه نفس الذات وزعمت المعتزلة أنه صفة، والقول بإثبات كون المعدوم شيئاً بناء على هذا.
والذي أختاره تفريعا على القول بالحال أن ذلك باطل؛ لأن الذوات لو كانت مشتركة سواء قلنا الوجود الذات على ما يقوله أصحابنا أو غيره على ما يقوله المعتزلة لصح على كل واحد ما يصح على الآخر ضرورة استواء المماثلات في كل اللوازم، لمكان يلزم صحة انقلاب القديم محدثا والجوهر عرضاً وبالعكس وهو محال؛ ولأن اختصاص الذات المعينة بالصفة المعينة إن كان لا لأمر فقد ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح وهو محال، وإن كان الأمر فذاك الأمر إن كان ذاتا عاد البحث في اختصاصها دون سائر الذوات بصفة المرجحية وإن لم يكن ذاتا كان صفة لذات فيعود البحث في اختصاص تلك الذات بها، أما إذا جعلنا الخصوصية ذاتا وما به الاشتراك صفة اندفع الإشكال لأن الأشياء المختلفة مجوز اشتراكها في لازم واحد، وأما الأشياء المتساوية فلا يجوز اختلافها في اللوازم.
Branching on the doctrine of al-hal (state): They said the being-a-state of a thing is either caused by an existent subsisting in that thing, like being-knowledgeable caused by knowledge, or not so, like the blackness of black. The first is the caused state, the second the uncaused state. They agreed that existents are equal in essences (dhawat) and differ in these states. As for existence (wujud), the affirmers of states among us claimed it is the essence itself, while the Mu'tazila claimed it is an attribute. The claim of affirming that the non-existent is a thing is built on this. What I choose, branching on the doctrine of al-hal, is that this is false; because if essences were shared—whether we say existence is the essence as our companions hold, or otherwise as the Mu'tazila hold—then what is true of one would be true of the other by necessity of the equality of similars in all concomitants; hence the transformation of the eternal into originated and the substance into accident, and vice versa, would be valid—which is impossible. And because the specification of a particular essence with a particular attribute, if not due to something, then one side of the possible would be preponderated without a preponderator—impossible. And if due to something, then if that thing is an essence, the inquiry returns to its specification over other essences with the attribute of preponderance; and if not an essence, it is an attribute of an essence, so the inquiry returns to the specification of that essence with it. But if we make particularity the essence and that by which participation occurs the attribute, the problem is repelled because different things may share a single concomitant, but equal things cannot differ in concomitants.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of al-hal (state) by Imam Razi. He begins by presenting the doctrine: states are of two types, caused (like knowledge causing being-knowledgeable) and uncaused (like the blackness of black itself). Adherents agree that existents are equal in essence but differ in states. Regarding existence, some (like Ash'aris) say it is the essence, Mu'tazila say it is an attribute. The claim that the non-existent is a thing is based on this view. Razi then states his chosen refutation: if essences were shared (i.e., existence is common), then whatever applies to one essence would apply to another, leading to absurdities like the eternal becoming originated. He argues that if a specific essence gets a specific attribute without cause, that is preponderance without preponderator. If there is a cause, that cause either is an essence (regress) or an attribute of an essence (regress). The solution is to make particularity the essence and commonality the attribute, thus explaining how different entities can share some properties but not others.

Key Terms

معلل

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of an uncaused state?
A) Being knowledgeable caused by knowledge
B) The blackness of black
C) Existence as an attribute
D) The equality of essences

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of al-hal distinguishes between states that depend on an attribute and those that do not, and it links to the debate on whether existence is essence or attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence is the same as others in kind, but your states make you distinct, what states are you actively cultivating that will matter in the sight of Allah? Will you let uncaused states (like inherent temperament) define you, or will you pursue caused states (like knowledge) that you are responsible for?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that non-existents are 'things' because existence is an attribute. This means that even possibilities in Allah's knowledge have a kind of reality. How does this expand your understanding of Allah's omniscience, and does it increase your awe that He knows what could have been?
?
The Ash'aris say existence is identical to essence—so your very being is nothing but you. If your existence is not an extra layer, then your actions and states define you completely. Are you content with who you are becoming, or does this thought drive you to change?

Imam Razi's Refutation of al-Hal
Imam Razi refutes the doctrine of al-hal via two arguments and offers an alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's two main arguments against the doctrine of al-hal: the argument from equality of similars and the argument about specification without a preponderator.

Core Idea
If essences are shared, absurdities follow; if specification is arbitrary, it is impossible.

Having laid out the doctrine of al-hal, Imam Razi now refutes it decisively. He presents two independent arguments that show the doctrine leads to logical contradictions or infinite regress. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step, including his proposed solution to avoid the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Note how Razi begins with 'What I choose... is that this is false.'
He is about to argue that the doctrine of states is false.
2
Understand the first argument: equality of similars
Razi argues that if all essences share the same existence, then anything true of one essence would be true of all, leading to impossible transformations (eternal becoming originated, etc.).
This shows shared essences are contradictory.
3
Understand the second argument: specification without preponderator
If a specific essence gets a specific attribute without cause, that is preponderance without a preponderator (impossible). If there is a cause, it leads to an infinite regress.
This shows that specification cannot be explained by states.
4
Grasp Razi's own solution
Razi proposes that particularity (khassiyya) is the essence, and commonality is the attribute. This avoids the problem because different things can share attributes, but equal things (same essence) cannot differ in attributes.
This is an alternative to the doctrine of states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفريع على القول بالحال
قالوا ثبوت الحالية للشيء إما أن يكون معللا بموجود قائم بذلك الشيء كالعالمية المعللة بالعلم أو لا يكون كذلك كسوادية السواد، والأول هو الحال المعلل والثاني الحال الغير المعلل، واتفقوا على أن الموجودات متساوية في الذوات ومختلفة في هذه الأحوال. وأما الوجود فزعم مثبتو الحال منا أنه نفس الذات وزعمت المعتزلة أنه صفة، والقول بإثبات كون المعدوم شيئاً بناء على هذا.
والذي أختاره تفريعا على القول بالحال أن ذلك باطل؛ لأن الذوات لو كانت مشتركة سواء قلنا الوجود الذات على ما يقوله أصحابنا أو غيره على ما يقوله المعتزلة لصح على كل واحد ما يصح على الآخر ضرورة استواء المماثلات في كل اللوازم، لمكان يلزم صحة انقلاب القديم محدثا والجوهر عرضاً وبالعكس وهو محال؛ ولأن اختصاص الذات المعينة بالصفة المعينة إن كان لا لأمر فقد ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح وهو محال، وإن كان الأمر فذاك الأمر إن كان ذاتا عاد البحث في اختصاصها دون سائر الذوات بصفة المرجحية وإن لم يكن ذاتا كان صفة لذات فيعود البحث في اختصاص تلك الذات بها، أما إذا جعلنا الخصوصية ذاتا وما به الاشتراك صفة اندفع الإشكال لأن الأشياء المختلفة مجوز اشتراكها في لازم واحد، وأما الأشياء المتساوية فلا يجوز اختلافها في اللوازم.
Branching on the doctrine of al-hal (state): They said the being-a-state of a thing is either caused by an existent subsisting in that thing, like being-knowledgeable caused by knowledge, or not so, like the blackness of black. The first is the caused state, the second the uncaused state. They agreed that existents are equal in essences (dhawat) and differ in these states. As for existence (wujud), the affirmers of states among us claimed it is the essence itself, while the Mu'tazila claimed it is an attribute. The claim of affirming that the non-existent is a thing is built on this. What I choose, branching on the doctrine of al-hal, is that this is false; because if essences were shared—whether we say existence is the essence as our companions hold, or otherwise as the Mu'tazila hold—then what is true of one would be true of the other by necessity of the equality of similars in all concomitants; hence the transformation of the eternal into originated and the substance into accident, and vice versa, would be valid—which is impossible. And because the specification of a particular essence with a particular attribute, if not due to something, then one side of the possible would be preponderated without a preponderator—impossible. And if due to something, then if that thing is an essence, the inquiry returns to its specification over other essences with the attribute of preponderance; and if not an essence, it is an attribute of an essence, so the inquiry returns to the specification of that essence with it. But if we make particularity the essence and that by which participation occurs the attribute, the problem is repelled because different things may share a single concomitant, but equal things cannot differ in concomitants.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of al-hal (state) by Imam Razi. He begins by presenting the doctrine: states are of two types, caused (like knowledge causing being-knowledgeable) and uncaused (like the blackness of black itself). Adherents agree that existents are equal in essence but differ in states. Regarding existence, some (like Ash'aris) say it is the essence, Mu'tazila say it is an attribute. The claim that the non-existent is a thing is based on this view. Razi then states his chosen refutation: if essences were shared (i.e., existence is common), then whatever applies to one essence would apply to another, leading to absurdities like the eternal becoming originated. He argues that if a specific essence gets a specific attribute without cause, that is preponderance without preponderator. If there is a cause, that cause either is an essence (regress) or an attribute of an essence (regress). The solution is to make particularity the essence and commonality the attribute, thus explaining how different entities can share some properties but not others.

Key Terms
اشتراك
لوازم
مرجح
مماثلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think states are real attributes distinct from essence that explain why essences differ.
Correction: Razi shows this leads to either absurd consequences or infinite regress; instead, particularity is the essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, what follows if essences are shared?
A) The eternal could become originated and substance could become accident
B) All things become identical
C) Attributes become unnecessary
D) The doctrine of states is proven

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the doctrine of al-hal by showing that shared essences imply absurdities and that specification cannot be explained without infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the impossibility of arbitrary specification challenge your own assumptions about why you have certain traits or beliefs? Are there areas in your life where you rely on 'just because' rather than seeking a proper cause? Commit to identifying one such area today.
?
If the equality of similars in all concomitants is a rational necessity, what does that imply about your relationship with others who share the same humanity? Are you treating them as equal in what matters? Examine your actions today.
?
Razi argues that making particularity the essence solves the problem. How does this principle apply to your own identity? Are you grounding your distinctiveness in something essential or accidental? What change is required?

Refuting the Claim That Necessity Is Privative
Imam Razi refutes the claim that necessity is privative through multiple logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's logical arguments against the claim that necessity (wujūb) is a negative concept and why such a view is rationally untenable.

Core Idea
If necessity were negative, it could not strengthen existence—but it does, so it must be positive.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles a subtle objection: perhaps necessity is not a real attribute but merely the absence of possibility. He responds with multiple refutations, showing that necessity must be an existential attribute to have any causal role. This lesson will walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
The objector claims necessity is privative. Razi's first response: necessity strengthens existence, and a thing is not strengthened by its opposite.
Notice that Razi uses the intuitive principle that opposites do not strengthen each other.
2
Examine the second argument
Razi argues that if necessity were negative, it would necessitate non-necessity, which is non-existential. Since non-existence cannot be a cause, necessity must be existential.
This argument relies on the idea that a cause must be consistent with its effect.
3
Consider the concession
Razi grants for argument's sake that necessity is privative, but then shows that it cannot be the cause of existence because non-existence cannot cause existence.
Even if we accept the premise, the conclusion fails.
4
Understand the final answer
The entire objection is based on the assumption that existence is univocal between God and contingent beings, which has already been refuted.
This reframing shows the objection is moot given prior conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : الوجوب سلبى . لانا نقول إنه يتأكد الوجود به والشيء لا يتأكد بنقيضه ، ولأنه يقتضى اللا وجوب الذي هو عدمى لكونه محمولا على العدم فيكون وجوديا ، سلمنا كونه سلبيا لكن يستحيل أن يكون المقتضى للوجود هو الوجوب لامتناع كون العدم مقتضيا للوجود ولا بالعكس ، وإلا كان كل موجود واجبا . والجواب : أنه بناء على كون الوجود مشتركا بين الواجب والممكن وهو محال على ما تقدم
It cannot be said: Necessity is privative. For we say: It [necessity] strengthens existence, and a thing is not strengthened by its opposite; and because it necessitates non-necessity which is non-existential, since it is predicated of non-existence, so it becomes existential. We grant that it is privative, but it is impossible that the necessitator of existence is necessity, because it is impossible for non-existence to necessitate existence, nor the reverse; otherwise every existent would be necessary. And the answer: That is based on existence being shared between the Necessary and the possible, and that is impossible according to what has preceded.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that necessity (wujūb) is a negative concept (salbī). Imam Razi responds by arguing that if necessity were negative, it could not strengthen existence, as something cannot be strengthened by its opposite. He further argues that the objector's position leads to a contradiction: if necessity is negative, it supposedly necessitates non-necessity, which is non-existential; but since non-existence cannot necessitate existence, necessity must be existential. Even if one concedes that necessity is privative, it cannot be the cause of existence, because non-existence cannot cause existence. The final answer reframes the issue: the entire objection presupposes that existence is a univocal term between the Necessary and the contingent, which has already been shown to be impossible.

Key Terms
وجوب
سلبى
وجود
مشترك
واجب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think necessity is a negative concept (e.g., the absence of possibility), implying it is not a real attribute.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that necessity must be positive because it strengthens existence, and because it necessitates non-necessity (a non-existential) making it existential. Even if granted as privative, it cannot cause existence.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why can't necessity (wujūb) be a purely negative concept?
A) Because negative concepts cannot be predicated of anything.
B) Because it strengthens existence, and a thing cannot be strengthened by its opposite.
C) Because all attributes of God must be positive.
D) Because the objector's argument is logically flawed regardless of the nature of necessity.

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be a negative attribute because it strengthens existence and necessitates non-necessity, proving it is existential; moreover, the objection presupposes a univocal concept of existence that has already been refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God's attributes as merely 'what He is not'—like infinite, eternal, necessary—as if they are just negations of human limitations? How does Razi's argument that necessity strengthens existence force you to reconsider the positive reality of God's attributes? What would change in your worship if you truly believed that every attribute of God is a positive, real perfection?
?
This lesson denies that existence is the same for God and for you. In your daily life, do you treat the existence of God as if it were just a bigger or better version of your own existence? How might the belief that existence is not univocal affect the way you relate to God—not as a being among beings, but as the very ground of being?
?
Razi says if necessity were merely negative, it could not cause anything—including your own existence. If you have ever doubts about how an unseen God can act in the world, does this argument give you a reason to affirm that God's attributes are real and effective? What is the one thing you will do today to remind yourself that God's necessity is a positive, active reality and not just a concept?

Definition of al-Mumkin and the First Objection to Imkan
Razi defines al-mumkin and presents the first objection against imkan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the possible (al-mumkin) in kalam and the first objection raised against the concept of imkan, along with a response that clarifies its meaning.

Core Idea
The possible is not self-evident; it faces logical puzzles that early kalam scholars had to resolve.

This lesson introduces a foundational concept in Islamic theology: the possible (al-mumkin). Imam Razi defines it as that which neither existence nor non-existence entails impossibility. However, this definition immediately attracts a sophisticated objection that questions whether 'possibility' can be coherently attributed at all. We will work through the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-mumkin
Start by reading the Arabic definition carefully. Notice that it is a conditional statement: something is possible if assuming its existence or non-existence does not lead to a logical contradiction.
This definition is the standard one in kalam.
2
Follow the first objection
The objection is presented as a dilemma. Take blackness as an example: is its existence identical to its essence or not? Investigate both horns and see why each leads to absurdity if 'possibility' is taken in a certain sense.
The objection aims to show that the concept of possibility is incoherent.
3
Understand the response
The response (by the author or commentator) argues that the objection confuses different views on essence and existence. It clarifies what 'possible' means for those who hold existence identical to essence and for those who distinguish them.
The response dismisses the objection as relevant only to sophists.
4
Reflect on the key lesson
Consider why this objection matters: it forces us to be precise about the relationship between essence and existence, a central issue in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(مسألة : الممكن لذاته هو الذي لا يلزم من فرض وجوده ولا من فرض عدمه من حيث هو محال) فإن قبل القول بالإمكان ممتنع من وجوه . الأول : أن وجود السواد مثلا إما أن يكون عين كونه سواداً أو غيره فإن كان الأول كان قولك السواد يصح أن يكون موجوداً ويصح أن يكون معدوما جاريا مجرى قولنا الوجود يصح أن يكون موجوداً و أن يكون معدوما ، لكن قولنا يصح أن يكون موجودا باطل ؛ لأن الموجود الذي جعلناه موضوعا [؟] والذي جعلناه محمولا إن كان واحدا كان ذلك إضافة للشيء إلى نفسه بالإمكان وهو محال ، وإن لم يكن واحدا لزم كون الشيء الواحد موجودا مرتين وأما قولنا الموجود يصح أن يكون معدوما فباطل أيضاً ، لأنه إذا حكم على أمر بأنه يصح اتصافه بأمر آخر فذلك يستدعي إمكان تقرر الموصوف مع الوصف والموجودية لا يعقل تقررها مع المعدومية فيستحيل أن يكون المحكوم عليه بصحة المعدم نفس الموجود وأما إن كان الحق هو الثاني كان قولنا السواد يمكن أن يكون موجوداً يرجع حاصله إلى أن المعدوم يمكن أن يصير موصوفا بالوجود وذلك محال على ما تقدم ، ولأنه إذا كان الوجود غير الماهية فالموصوف بالإمكان إما الوجود وإما الماهية وإما موصوفية الماهية بالوجود ، وأي هذه الثلاثة فرض الإمكان وصفا له فذلك الموصوف بالإمكان إما أن يكون مفرداً أو مركبا ، فإن كان الحكم عليه بالإمكان يرجع إلى تلك الماهية المفردة لكان معنى الحكم عليها بالإمكان أن تلك الماهية المفردة يمكن أن تكون تلك الماهية [؟] ويمكن أن لا تكون فيعود إلى التقسيم الأول الذي أبطلناه ، وإن كان مركبا هذا الكلام في أن الإمكان صفة لكل واحد من أجزائه أو لبعض أجزائه على ما تقدم (1) أقول : هذا الإشكال لو أضافه إلى ما ذكره في صدر الكتاب من السفسطة لكان أليق ، وذلك لأن القائل بكون الوجود عين الماهية يريد بقوله السواد يصح أن يكون موجوداً ويصح أن يكون معدوما ، إذ من الممكن أن يحدث ما يسمى بعد حدوثه سواداً ويصح أن السواد ينعدم مطلقا ، وأما عند من يقول بتغاير السواد والوجود فليس يرجع حاصله إلى أن المعدوم يمكن أن يصير موصوفا بالوجود وهو معدوم فإن صاحب الكتاب يعترف من قريب بأن الماهية وحدها لا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة ولا واحدة ولا كثيرة، فالسواد من حيث هو سواد لا يكون معدوما . وقوله : المعدوم يمكن أن يصير موصوفا بالوجود معناه أن الماهية الموصوفة بالعدم يمكن أن يزول عنها المعدومية ويحدث بعدها لها صفة الوجود ، وإن السواد يمكن أن يوجد معناه أن الماهية التي لا يعبر معها وجود ولا عدم يمكن أن ينضاف إليها صفة الوجود ، وما في الكلام خيط ظاهر .
(Issue: The possible in itself is that which does not entail from the assumption of its existence nor from the assumption of its non-existence, in itself, an impossibility.) Then it is said that the claim of possibility is impossible from [several] aspects. The first: that the existence of blackness, for example, is either identical to its being blackness or other than it. If it is the first, then your saying 'blackness can exist and can be non-existent' runs in the course of our saying 'existence can exist and can be non-existent', but our saying 'it can exist' is false; because the existent which we made the subject and which we made the predicate, if it is one, that would be an attribution of the thing to itself with possibility, which is absurd; and if it is not one, then it entails that one thing exists twice. And our saying 'the existent can be non-existent' is also false, because if one judges of a thing that it can be qualified by another thing, that requires the possibility of the existence of the qualified together with the quality, and existence is not intelligible to exist together with non-existence, so it is impossible that the subject of the judgment of the possibility of non-existence is the very existent. And if the truth is the second [i.e., existence is other than essence], then our saying 'blackness can exist' amounts to saying that the non-existent can become qualified by existence, and that is impossible as previously established. And because if existence is other than essence, then that which is qualified by possibility is either existence, or essence, or the qualification of essence by existence. Whichever of these three is supposed to have possibility as its description, then that which is qualified by possibility is either simple or composite. If the judgment of possibility applies to that simple essence, then the meaning of the judgment of possibility upon it is that that simple essence can be that essence and can not be, so it returns to the first division which we invalidated. And if it is composite, this discourse is about whether possibility is a description of each of its parts or some of its parts, as previously. (1) I say: This objection, if he had added it to what he mentioned in the beginning of the book about sophistry, would have been more appropriate. That is because the one who holds that existence is identical to essence means by his saying 'blackness can exist and can be non-existent' that it is possible for something to occur which, after its occurrence, is called blackness, and it is possible that blackness ceases to exist absolutely. But according to the one who holds the difference between blackness and existence, his statement does not amount to saying that the non-existent can become qualified by existence while it is non-existent, for the author admits shortly before that the essence alone is neither existent nor non-existent nor one nor many; so blackness, inasmuch as it is blackness, is not non-existent. And his statement 'the non-existent can become qualified by existence' means that the essence qualified by non-existence can have non-existence removed from it and thereafter it acquires the attribute of existence. And 'blackness can exist' means that the essence which is not accompanied by existence nor non-existence can have the attribute of existence added to it. And there is no apparent defect in the discourse.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage opens with a definition of the possible (al-mumkin) and then presents the first of several objections to the very notion of possibility (al-imkan). The definition states that the possible in itself is something whose existence or non-existence, when assumed, does not entail an impossibility. Then Razi reports an objection: the claim of possibility is impossible from multiple aspects. The first aspect (al-wajh al-awwal) is a dilemma: take blackness, and consider whether its existence is identical to its essence (being black) or distinct. If identical, then saying 'blackness can exist' becomes like saying 'existence can exist', which is either a self-attribution (absurd) or a duplication of existence (also absurd). Similarly, 'existence can be non-existent' is impossible because existence cannot coexist with non-existence. If existence is distinct from essence, then 'blackness can exist' means a non-existent thing can acquire existence, which Razi claims is impossible. He then considers the subject of possibility: it could be existence, essence, or the essence's being qualified by existence. Each alternative leads to a regress or contradiction. The marginal comment (by a commentator, likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi himself or a student) responds that this objection is better directed against sophists. He clarifies that for those who hold existence identical to essence, 'blackness can exist' means a future black thing can come to be; for those who distinguish them, it means that the essence (which is neither existent nor non-existent in itself) can receive existence. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الممكن
الإمكان
الوجود
الماهية
العدم
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'possibility' is a simple, unproblematic concept that can be applied without further analysis.
Correction: As Razi shows, the concept of possibility involves deep issues about the relationship between essence and existence, and neglecting these leads to logical puzzles.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what problem arises if existence is identical to essence?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) Saying 'existence can exist' either attributes a thing to itself or duplicates existence.
C) It contradicts the definition of the possible.
D) It makes the possible impossible by definition.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to imkan forces us to clarify the relationship between essence and existence, a key theme in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that even the concept of 'possible' is not self-evident. When you make plans for tomorrow, do you truly understand what you are assuming? How does this realization humble your confidence in your own abilities and redirect your reliance on Allah, the Only Necessary Being? Will you now start each day by acknowledging that your very existence is a gift from Him?
?
The objection forces a choice: either existence is identical to essence or it is not. In your own life, when you say 'I can change', are you treating your essence as something that can receive a new attribute? Does this logical puzzle reveal that your self-understanding is fragile? How does this affect your desire for self-improvement—do you see it as something you achieve or something Allah grants?
?
The marginal commentator says that the essence is neither existent nor non-existent. Before you were born, your soul was merely possible. Now that you exist, do you take your existence for granted? This passage suggests that the very notion of possibility is fraught with difficulties. Will you use this to increase your gratitude for being brought from non-existence into existence, and to remember that you could be returned to non-existence at any moment?

The Second Objection: Dilemma of Existent or Non-Existent and Its Critique
Razi presents the second objection against possibility, followed by a critique of its divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against possibility (al-imkan) and how the commentator responds by showing the division is not exhaustive and the impossibility is conditional.

Core Idea
The objection fails because the division into existent/non-existent overlooks essence considered in itself.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the concept of possibility: everything is either existent or non-existent, and neither state allows for genuine possibility. The commentator shows that the division is incomplete because an essence can be considered apart from existence or non-existence. Understanding this critique is key to grasping the deeper logic of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where Razi presents the dilemma: the subject of possibility must be either existent or non-existent, and both cases preclude possibility. Notice how the argument is structured.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
2
Examine the alternative formulation
Razi then offers a second version: if the cause of existence is present, the thing is necessary; if absent, it is impossible. This reinforces the objection.
3
Study the commentator's response
Read the marginal comment which points out that the initial division (existent/non-existent) is not exhaustive. There is a third possibility: the essence itself, considered without either. Also, the impossibility in the first case is conditional (bi-shart al-mahmul), not absolute.
This is the key insight that saves possibility.
4
Analyze the critique of the second formulation
The comment also finds a flaw: 'not present' is ambiguous, and the division is incomplete. The objection assumes a false dichotomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن المحكوم عليه بالإمكان إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً ، فإن كان موجوداً فهو حال الوجود لا يقبل العدم لاستحالة الجمع بين الوجود والعدم، وإذا امتنع حصول العدم امتنع حصول إمكان الوجود والعدم، وإن كان معدوماً فهو حال العدم لا يقبل الوجود فلا يحصل إمكان الوجود والعدم ، وإذا استحال الخلو عن الوجود والعدم وكان كل واحد منهما منافيا للامكان كان القول بالإمكان محالا. يمكن تقرير هذا السؤال من وجه آخر : وهو أن الممكن إما أن يكون قد حضر معه سبب وجوده أو لم يحضر ، وبالتقدير الأول يجب وبالتقدير الثانى يمتنع فيكون القول بالإمكان ممتنعا (1) أقول : القسمة فى قوله المحكوم عليه بالإمكان إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً ليست بحاصرة ، لان المفهوم منه أن المحكوم عليه بالإمكان إما أن يكون مع الوجود أو مع العدم ويعوزه قسم آخر وهو أن لا يكون مع أحدهما . وأما قوله : فإن كان موجوداً فهو حال الوجود لا يقبل العدم ، يقال له هذا مسلم ، أما في غير تلك الحال فلم لا يقبل الوجود وليس حال الماهية أما حال الوجود أو حال العدم، لأن هذين الحالين عند اعتبار الماهية مع الغير يمكن أن يقبل أحدهما لا بعينه ، وهذا الامتناع امتناع لاحق بشرط المحمول . وفي التقرير الثانى الذى قال لهيه إن الممكن إما أن يحضر معه سبب وجوده أو لم يحضر أيضاً فيه خلل ؛ لأن لم يحضر يحتمل أن يحضر معه لم يحضر سبب وجوده ، أو لم يحضر لا بسبب وجوده ، ولا لم يحضر سبب وجوده الذي هو سبب عدمه ، فظهر أن الخلل في هذا الكلام كان سبب أن القسمة لم تكن مستوفاة .
And the second: that the subject of the judgment of possibility is either existent or non-existent. If it is existent, then in the state of existence it cannot accept non-existence because of the impossibility of combining existence and non-existence; and if the occurrence of non-existence is impossible, then the occurrence of the possibility of existence and non-existence is impossible. And if it is non-existent, then in the state of non-existence it cannot accept existence, so the possibility of existence and non-existence does not obtain. And if it is impossible to be free from existence and non-existence, and each of them is incompatible with possibility, then the claim of possibility is impossible. This question can be formulated from another direction: that the possible either has the cause of its existence present with it or not; on the first supposition it is necessary, and on the second it is impossible, so the claim of possibility becomes impossible. (1) I say: The division in his statement 'the subject of the judgment of possibility is either existent or non-existent' is not exhaustive, because what is understood from it is that the subject of the judgment of possibility is either with existence or with non-existence, but it lacks another division: that it is with neither of them. As for his statement 'if it is existent, then in the state of existence it cannot accept non-existence', it is said to him: this is conceded, but why does it not accept existence in a state other than that state? And the state of the essence is not either the state of existence or the state of non-existence, because these two states, when considering the essence with something else, it can accept one of them indeterminately. This impossibility is an impossibility that follows from the condition of the predicate. And in the second formulation which he mentioned, that the possible either has the cause of its existence present with it or not, there is also a flaw; because 'not present' could mean that the cause of its existence is not present with it, or that it is not present without a cause of existence, or that the cause of its existence which is the cause of its non-existence is not present. So it is clear that the flaw in this discourse was that the division was not exhaustive.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (al-wajh al-thani) against the concept of possibility. The objection is structured as a dilemma: the subject of the judgment of possibility (the thing said to be possible) must be either existent or non-existent. If it is existent, then in its current state of existence it cannot accept non-existence (since existence and non-existence cannot coexist). If it cannot accept non-existence, then the possibility of both existence and non-existence (which is what possibility means) is impossible. If it is non-existent, then in its current state of non-existence it cannot accept existence, so again possibility is impossible. Since everything must be either existent or non-existent, and neither state allows possibility, the claim of possibility is impossible. The objection is then reformulated: if the possible has its cause present, it is necessary; if not, it is impossible — so possibility is eliminated. The marginal comment responds that the initial division (existent/non-existent) is not exhaustive, because the essence can be considered in itself without either existence or non-existence. The commentator argues that the impossibility of combining existence and non-existence is a conditional impossibility (imtina' bi-shart al-mahmul), not an absolute one. The second formulation is also flawed because 'not present' is ambiguous and the division is incomplete.

الموجود
المعدوم
الخلو
الامتناع
القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everything is either existent or non-existent in an absolute sense.
Correction: An essence can be considered in itself without either existence or non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal comment, what is the flaw in the second objection's initial division?
A) The division is not exhaustive because essence can be considered without existence or non-existence.
B) The division confuses necessary and contingent existence.
C) The objection relies on a false assumption about causes.
D) The objection assumes possibility is a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
The second objection against possibility fails because its division into existent and non-existent is not exhaustive, and the impossibility it relies on is conditional, not absolute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a major decision or uncertainty in your life, do you tend to think of it as already fixed (existent) or impossible (non-existent)? How does ignoring the essence of the matter itself—apart from current circumstances—lead you to miss genuine possibilities that Allah has placed before you? Take a specific example and commit to examining it from the perspective of the essence, not just its current state.
?
The objection assumes that possibility requires a subject that is either existent or non-existent, but the commentator shows that possibility is about the essence itself. In your own faith, do you sometimes reduce Allah's attributes or decrees to only what is currently manifest, forgetting that His power and wisdom encompass essences beyond our current experience? How can you cultivate a mindset that trusts in Allah's possibilities beyond what you see?
?
This lesson reveals a common logical error: an incomplete division. In your own thinking about right and wrong or about religious rulings, are there times when you assume a false dichotomy (e.g., either this action is obligatory or forbidden, missing the middle categories like recommended or permissible)? How can you train yourself to seek comprehensive understanding before judging?

The Third Objection: Is Possibility a Negative or Positive Attribute?
Razi presents the third objection and the marginal comment clarifies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the reality of possibility and why the infinite regress argument fails when possibility is understood as an intellectual consideration.

Core Idea
Possibility is an intellectual tool, not a real attribute — so the regress dissolves.

This lesson examines a sophisticated objection to the concept of possibility. The objection tries to force possibility into being either a negative or positive attribute, both of which lead to absurdities. The marginal comment then rescues the concept by clarifying that possibility is an intellectual consideration, not a real existential quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the dilemma
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection presents two horns: possibility is either a negative attribute or a positive one. Both are argued to be false.
The negative option is rejected quickly; the positive option is attacked with two arguments.
2
Understand why the negative option fails
The objection says if possibility were negative, it would be the contradictory of impossibility, which is predicated of non-existents. But one of the two contradictories must be positive, so possibility must be positive.
This forces the opponent to accept possibility as a positive attribute.
3
Analyze the arguments against the positive option
The passage gives two arguments: (1) If possibility is positive, the possible would become necessary; (2) if the act of qualification is possible, an infinite regress follows. Focus on the regress argument.
The marginal comment responds specifically to the regress argument.
4
Grasp the commentator's resolution
The comment says possibility is an intellectual matter (amr 'aqli). The regress only occurs as long as the intellect considers it, and stops when consideration ceases. This dissolves the regress.
This shows that possibility is not a real attribute but a conceptual tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالها : وهو أن الشى لو كان ممكنا لكان إمكانه إما أن يكون وصفا عدميا أو وجوديا ، والأول باطل ؛ لأنه نقيض للا إمكان الذي يصح حمله على المعدوم ، والمحمول على المعدوم معدوم فيكون الإمكان ثبوتيا ضرورة كون أحد النقيضين وجودياً . والثانى باطل ؛ لأنه لو كان ثبوتيا لزم المحال من وجهين : الأول : أنه إذا كان ثبوتيا كان مساويا لسائر الموجودات في أصل الثبوت و مخالفا لها في خصوصية ماهيته المسماة بالإمكان فيكون ثبوته زائداً على ماهيته، فاتصاف ماهيته بوجوده إن كان واجباً لذاته كان الإمكان موجوداً واجباً لذاته وهو صفة الممكن والموصوف بالوجود موجود فالممكن موجود ووجوده شرط لقيام ذلك الإمكان به وما كان شرط الوجود ما كان واجباً لذاته كان أولى أن يكون واجباً لذاته فالممكن لذاته واجب لذاته هذا خلف ، وأما إن كان اتصاف ماهيته بوجوده على سبيل الإمكان كان الإمكان إمكان آخر ولام أن يكون إمكان الإمكان زائداً عليه ولزم التسلسل (۲) أقول ما فى قوله فى إبطال كون الإمكان عدميا فما تبين حاله . وقوله فى الوجه الأول من إبطال كونه ثبوتيا أنه لو كان ممكنا لكان اتصاف ماهيته بوجوده على سبيل الإمكان وكان الإمكان إمكان آخر ولزم التسلسل ليس بحق ، لأن الإمكان أمر عقلى فمهما اعتبر العقل للإمكان ماهية ووجوداً حصل فيه إمكان إمكان وإنقطع عند انقطاع اعتباره . وههنا نكتة ينبغى أن تحصل وهو أن كون الشيء معقولا ينظر فيه العقل ويعتبر وجوده ولا وجوده غير كونه آلة للعاقل لا ينظر فيه حيث ينظر فيما هو آلة لتعقله ، بل إنما ينظر به مثلا العاقل يعقل السماء ولا يحكم عليها بحكم بل يعقل أن المعقول بتلك الصورة هو السماء وهو جوهر ، ثم إذا نظر في تلك الصورة أي يجعلها
The third: that if a thing were possible, its possibility would either be a negative description or a positive description. The first is false because it is the opposite of impossibility which can be predicated of the non-existent, and what is predicated of the non-existent is non-existent, so possibility is positive by necessity, since one of the two contradictories is positive. The second is also false, because if it were positive, an absurdity would follow from two aspects: The first: if it is positive, it is equal to other existents in the principle of subsistence and differs from them in the specificity of its essence called possibility, so its subsistence is additional to its essence. Then the qualification of its essence by its existence, if it is necessary in itself, then possibility would be existent and necessary in itself — and it is an attribute of the possible, and that which is qualified by existence is existent, so the possible is existent — and its existence is a condition for that possibility to subsist in it, and what is a condition for existence, if it is necessary in itself, is more entitled to be necessary in itself, so the possible in itself is necessary in itself — this is a contradiction. And if the qualification of its essence by its existence is by way of possibility, then possibility would be another possibility, and it would be necessary for the possibility of possibility to be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. (2) I say: As for his statement in refuting that possibility is negative, its status is not clear [or: it has not been clarified]. And his statement in the first aspect of refuting that it is positive, that if it were possible then its essence would be qualified by its existence by way of possibility, and possibility would be another possibility, and an infinite regress would follow — this is not correct, because possibility is an intellectual matter; whenever the intellect considers a possibility as an essence and existence, there obtains in it a possibility of possibility, and it ceases when its consideration ceases. And here there is a subtlety that should be grasped: that the thing being intelligible, the intellect considers it and takes its existence and non-existence, not that it is an instrument for the intellect to look into where it looks into what is an instrument for its intellection, but rather it looks by means of it. For example, the intellect intellects the heaven and does not judge it with a judgment, but rather intellection that the intelligible in that form is the heaven and it is a substance; then if it looks into that form, that is, it makes it...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (al-wajh al-thalith) against possibility. The objection argues that possibility must be either a negative attribute (like 'not impossible') or a positive attribute (an existent quality). The first option (negative) is rejected because it is the contradictory of impossibility, which is predicated of non-existents; when a predicate applies to a non-existent, the predicate itself is non-existent, so possibility would be non-existent — but it must be positive because one of two contradictories is positive. The second option (positive) is then attacked from two angles: (1) If possibility is a positive attribute, it is an existent and its existence is additional to its essence (the attribute 'possibility' is distinct from the thing's essence). Then the thing's essence would be qualified by existence either necessarily (making the possible necessary) or possibly (leading to another possibility and an infinite regress). (2) The second argument is the regress: if the essence is qualified by existence through possibility, then that possibility itself would need another possibility, and so on. The marginal comment responds that the refutation of the negative option is not clear. Regarding the regress argument, the commentator says it is not correct because possibility is an intellectual consideration; the regress only arises as long as the intellect considers it, and it stops when consideration stops. He introduces a subtlety: when the intellect considers something, it uses that thing as an instrument of thought, not as an object of thought in the same act. The example of intellecting heaven illustrates this distinction.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الوصف العدمي
الوصف الوجودي
التسلسل
الأمر العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility must be a real attribute in the external world, otherwise it cannot be rationally discussed.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that possibility is an intellectual consideration (amr 'aqli). It does not need to be a real existential quality to be a valid concept; the regress argument only arises if one insists on treating it as a real attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal comment, why does the infinite regress argument against positive possibility fail?
A) Because possibility is not a real attribute but an intellectual consideration.
B) Because possibility is necessary by definition.
C) Because the objection confuses essence and existence.
D) Because the regress is inevitable and therefore acceptable.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is an intellectual consideration; the regress argument fails because it only exists as long as the intellect maintains it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself trapped in endless doubts or overthinking about your faith, like an infinite regress of 'what if'? How does knowing that some things are only intellectual considerations free you from that infinite cycle, and what step will you take today to stop the regress and rest in what the intellect ultimately settles on?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you sometimes worry that your human logic might lead to contradictions or absurdities? This lesson shows that some attributes are purely intellectual tools—like possibility—and not real features of God. How does that change your approach when you feel your intellect overwhelmed? Will you now trust that your reasoning is valid as long as you keep the distinction between intellectual and real, or will you retreat from rational inquiry altogether?

Answering the First Objection: The Taqsim Argument Backfires
Imam al-Razi refutes the first objection by applying the division argument to a self-evident truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector's claim that the taqsim argument undermines proofs is refuted by showing it can be applied to self-evident truths, leading to absurdities.

Core Idea
The division argument, when applied to self-evident truths, proves too much and thus refutes itself.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi responds to the first objection that the division (taqsim) argument invalidates necessary knowledge. He demonstrates that the same method of division can be turned against a self-evident truth—'I exist now'—yielding absurd consequences. This shows that the objection is not a valid criticism but rather a confusion about how division arguments work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the division applied to a self-evident truth
The author applies the same kind of division used in the original proof to the proposition 'I am existing in this hour.' Find the branches: non-existential vs. existential, and within existential: identical to essence or additional.
This mirrors the structure of the original division argument.
3
Understand why the objection backfires
Each branch leads to an absurdity (impossibility, infinite regress). Since the self-evident truth is undeniable, the division argument itself must be invalid when applied in this way—thus the objection is refuted.
The objector's own weapon is turned against him.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how the author uses the opponent's method to show that it proves too much (would deny self-evident truths), so the objection is not a valid criticism.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعارضة الأولى : فمدفوعة ، لأن ذلك التقسيم قد يتوجه فيما يعلم وجوده بالضرورة كما لو قيل لو كنت أنا موجودا في هذه الساعة لكان كونى فيها إما أن يكون عدميا وهو محال ، لأنه نقيض اللاكون فيها وهو عدمى ونقيض العدم ثبوت، أو يكون ثبوتيا وهو إما عين الذات فيلزم أن لا تبقى الذات عندما يبقى حصوله في تلك الساعة أو زائداً عليه فيكون ذلك الزائد حاصلا في تلك الساعة وازم التسلسل ، ولما كان حصوله فى هذه الساعة يفضى إلى هذه الاقسام الباطلة وجب أن لا يكون له حصول في هذه الساعة فظهر أن هذا التقسيم مبطل للبديهيات .
As for the first objection, it is refuted, because that division may be directed at what is known by necessity, as if one were to say: If I am existing in this hour, then my being in it is either non-existential—which is impossible, because it is the opposite of not-being-in-it, which is non-existential, and the opposite of non-existence is positive—or it is existential, and it is either identical to the essence—then it would follow that the essence does not remain when its existence in that hour remains—or additional to it, then that addition would be existent in that hour and lead to infinite regress. Since its existence in this hour leads to these false divisions, it must not have existence in this hour. Thus it appears that this division invalidates self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is the response to the first objection (al-mu'arada al-ula). The author refutes the opponent's claim that the division argument (taqsim) used earlier undermines the proof. He shows that the same kind of division can be applied to a self-evident truth, such as 'I am existing in this hour.' The division yields absurdities (like non-existential being-in-time or infinite regress), which would force the conclusion that one does not exist at that hour. Since this conclusion is false, it reveals that such a division is actually a refutation of self-evident truths, not a valid objection. Thus the opponent's division argument backfires.

Key Terms
معارضة
تقسيم
بديهيات
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division arguments always undermine necessary truths.
Correction: The passage shows that division arguments can be applied to self-evident truths, but when they lead to absurd conclusions, it is the argument (or its application) that is flawed, not the truth itself.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the division argument when applied to 'I exist now'?
A) It confirms the truth of 'I exist now'.
B) It leads to absurdities, showing the argument invalidates self-evident truths.
C) It proves that existence is non-existential.
D) It demonstrates infinite regress is acceptable.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because the same division argument, when applied to self-evident truths, leads to absurdities, thus refuting the objector's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed a clever argument to make you doubt something you knew was true in your heart? How will you guard against that now?
?
This lesson shows that even self-evident truths can be made to seem false through flawed logic. When has your faith been shaken by doubts that, upon reflection, were based on flawed reasoning? What will you do next time doubt arises?
?
The author uses logical rigor to defend truth. Are you willing to put in the effort to understand the foundations of your beliefs, or do you prefer comfortable certainty? What step will you take today to strengthen your intellectual defense of truth?

Refuting Two Objections: Sound and Its Attributes
Refutation of two objections using divisive reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second and third objections against the occurrence of a sound are refuted by showing that the opponent's own division leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
The opponent's division either leads to contradiction or invalidates necessary truths.

Imam Razi continues his refutation of objections to the argument for a Creator. Here he addresses two further objections regarding the occurrence of a sound and its attributes. Both are refuted by turning the opponent's own method of division against them, revealing that the proposed alternatives either collapse into contradiction or undermine self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Familiarize yourself with the flow of the argument.
Notice that both objections are refuted using a similar pattern: the opponent's division leads to absurdity.
2
Analyze the second objection
Examine the second objection about ḥudūth (origination). The opponent says occurrence must be either at existence or non-existence. Identify why both options are contradictory.
If occurrence at existence, the existent already exists; if at non-existence, existence arises from nothing—impossible.
3
Analyze the third objection
The third objection concerns what actually occurs: essence, existence, or the attribute of being-described by existence. See how each option leads to a transformation of a non-thing into a thing.
4
Understand the significance
Realize that the divisions used by the opponents are presented as implying impossibility, thus invalidating their objections. This demonstrates the method of logical refutation (ilzām).
The author labels one as 'invalidating necessary truths' and the other as 'invalidating self-evident truths'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الثانية : فهى كذلك أيضا ، لأنه أحداث ، فإن كان في محل البحث ، اليك لا نزاع في الحدوث والتقسيم الذي ذكرتموه يدفعه لأنه يقال إن حدث هذا الصوت مثلا فإما أن يكون حدوثه حال وجوده أو حال عدمه ، فإن حدث حال وجوده فقد وجد الموجود وإن حدث حال عدمه فقد وجد عند عدمه ، فظهر أن هذا التقسيم مبطل الضروريات . وأما المعارضة الثالثة : فهي أيضاً كذلك لأنه يقال له : إن حدث هذا الصوت لكان الحادث. : إما الماهية أو الوجود أو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود ، فان كان الأول فقد انقلب ما ليس بصوت صوتا وإن كان الثاني فقد انقلب ما ليس بوجود وجودا وكذا الثالث فظهر أن هذا التقسيم مبطل للبديهيات
As for the second objection, it is likewise, because it concerns occurrences (ahdāth). If it is under discussion—no, there is no dispute about origination (al-ḥudūth)—the division you mentioned refutes it, because one says: If this sound occurs, for instance, then its occurrence is either at the time of its existence or at the time of its non-existence. If it occurs at its existence, then the existent has come to exist; if it occurs at its non-existence, then it has come to exist at its non-existence. Thus it appears that this division invalidates necessary truths (al-ḍarūriyyāt). As for the third objection, it is likewise, because one says to him: If this sound occurs, then the occurring thing is either the essence (al-māhiyya), or existence (al-wujūd), or the being-described of the essence by existence. If it is the first, then what is not a sound has turned into a sound; if the second, then what is not existence has turned into existence; likewise the third. Thus it appears that this division invalidates self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt).
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage refutes two separate objections (second and third) using the same method. For the second objection regarding origination (ḥudūth), the opponent's division (occurrence either at existence or non-existence) leads to absurdity: if occurrence happens at existence, existence already is; if at non-existence, existence arises at non-existence—contradiction. Thus the division undermines necessary truths. For the third objection about what actually occurs when a sound happens—whether the essence, existence, or the attribute of being-described—each option leads to a contradiction: a non-sound becomes sound, etc. So again the division is shown to invalidate self-evident truths. The author uses the opponent's own divisive reasoning against them.

Key Terms
حدوث
صوت
ماهية
وجود
موصوفية
ضروريات
بديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the occurrence of a sound can be explained by one of the three alternatives (essence, existence, or attribute) without contradiction.
Correction: The passage shows that each alternative leads to a logical contradiction, thus the division itself is invalid.

Checkpoint
In the second objection, what absurdity arises if the occurrence of a sound is said to happen at its non-existence?
A) The sound would be non-existent eternally.
B) The existent would come to exist at non-existence, which is contradictory.
C) The sound would be a preponderator.
D) The sound would be a cause.

Key Takeaway
The second and third objections are refuted by showing that the opponent's division leads to either logical contradiction or the invalidation of necessary truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical objection to your belief, do you immediately reject it or do you examine its assumptions? This lesson shows that an opponent's division can be turned against them. Have you ever used the same method to defend your own position? How certain are you that your reasoning is sound?
?
The passage insists on necessary truths (ḍarūriyyāt). Are there truths about Allah that you treat as necessary, or do you sometimes waver when faced with doubts? What would it take for you to become absolutely certain?
?
The author uses the opponent's own division to force a contradiction. Is there an area in your life where you hold to a division that actually leads to absurdity? Are you willing to let go of it and embrace a more coherent understanding?

The Problem of Damaging Self-Evident Truths and the Fourth Objection
Imam Razi addresses a problem and refutes the fourth objection about non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the challenge that could be raised against the taqsim argument regarding self-evident truths and the refutation of the fourth objection about non-existence.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths cannot be impugned, and non-existence needs no preponderator.

Imam Razi here confronts two potential difficulties with the preceding argument. First, he notes an opponent might claim that if the argument's premises are sound but its conclusion is false, then self-evident truths are damaged. Second, he refutes the claim that non-existence requires a preponderator by showing it is pure negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Imam Razi addresses two distinct issues in this short passage.
2
Identify the two issues
The passage presents a problem (shakl) about self-evident truths and then the fourth objection (muʿāraḍa رابعة). Identify each and see how they differ.
The first is a potential criticism of the overall argument; the second is a specific objection about non-existence.
3
Understand the refutation
Focus on why the fourth objection is dismissed: non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot have preponderance and thus needs no preponderator.
This refutation relies on the nature of non-existence as absolute non-being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا أشكال وهو أن للقادمين فى البديهيات أن يقولوا لما عجزتم عن القدح في مقدمات هذا التقسيم مع أنكم عليتم أن الميجته باطلة لزم منه تطرق القدح إلى البديهيات . وأما المعاوضة الرابعة : فمدفوعة لأن العدم نفى محض فيستحيل وصفه بالرجحان ، فلا جرم لا يفتقر إلى مرجح (1) .
And here is a problem: It is that those who reject self-evident truths may say: Since you are unable to find fault with the premises of this division, while you have concluded that the premise? is invalid, it follows that fault enters self-evident truths. As for the fourth objection, it is refuted because non-existence is pure negation, so it is impossible to describe it with preponderance (al-rujḥān), consequently it does not need a preponderator.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage covers two items: first, a potential problem (shakl) that an opponent might raise: if the author cannot find fault with the premises of the taqsim argument yet declares the conclusion false, then that would impugn self-evident truths. The author seems to present this as a challenge he will address (perhaps in the following text, but here he does not give a direct answer). Second, the fourth objection is straightforwardly refuted: non-existence (al-‘adam) is pure negation, so it cannot be described as being preponderant (rajḥān); hence it does not require a preponderator. This closes the series of objections.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non-existence can have weight or preponderance and therefore needs a cause or preponderator.
Correction: Non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be described as preponderant; thus it does not require a preponderator.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say non-existence does not need a preponderator?
A) Because it is a positive attribute.
B) Because it has no preponderance.
C) Because it is pure negation and cannot be described with preponderance.
D) Because it is a cause.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot be preponderant and needs no preponderator; the taqsim argument's premises remain sound, protecting self-evident truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that non-existence is 'pure negation' and needs no preponderator, does your mind instinctively treat non-existence as something that 'happens' or 'exists' in some way? Examine your heart: do you ever fear or attribute power to absence—like the absence of health, wealth, or life—as if it were a force itself? How would your reliance on Allah change if you truly believed that only what He brings into existence requires a cause, and every absence is simply His withholding?
?
This lesson shows that even self-evident truths can be called into question. Have you ever rejected a clear truth about Allah—such as His oneness or your own need of Him—simply because it was uncomfortable or because you wanted to avoid the implications? What self-evident truth are you hesitating to accept fully? Today, will you surrender your intellect to that truth, trusting that Allah does not command the impossible?
